ENG 101 - Week 1 Day 1 Class Schedule (SLAVE to File 1; SLAVE to File 2;)

Day 1 Schedule

I. Writing Warm-Up (15 minutes)

1. Purpose

a. A daily exercise to ease into writing, develop fluency, and build confidence in expressing thoughts.

2. Prompt Options

a. Concrete: Describe a time when you had to learn something new. What were the challenges?

b. Abstract: What does success mean to you?

c. Connecting: How do you think learning new skills shapes who you are today?

3. Instructions

a. Write freely for 10-15 minutes without overthinking.

b. Focus on thoughts rather than grammar or spelling.

c. Submission must be typed (Word or PDF only), no handwritten responses.

d. Submissions must be made within the first 20 minutes of class; late submissions will not be accepted.

e. Graded pass/fail, with 24 total points available for extra credit at the end of the term.

4. Discussion After Writing (5 minutes)

a. What challenges did you face in writing without stopping?

b. How does freewriting compare to your usual writing habits?



II. Proust Questionnaire Icebreaker (20 minutes)

1. Purpose

a. A fun and engaging activity to introduce students to self-reflection and deeper thinking about their writing identities.

2. Instructions

a. Answer questions that explore values, aspirations, and perspectives on life and writing.

b. Encourage open participation and sharing.

3. Sample Questions

a. What is your idea of perfect happiness?

b. Which words or phrases do you most overuse?

c. What talent would you most like to have?

4. Transition

a. Discuss how personal reflection informs writing style and voice.



III. Lecture: The Importance of Grammar, Punctuation, and Spelling (45 minutes)

1. Overview of the Weekâ€™s Theme

a. This week introduces the writing process, emphasizing grammar, punctuation, and spelling as foundational skills for effective communication. These elements are crucial for ensuring clarity, credibility, and professionalism in academic writing.

2. Key Concepts and Definitions

a. Grammar: The system and structure of a language, including syntax (sentence structure), morphology (word formation), and phonology (sound system). Proper grammar ensures clarity and coherence in communication.

1. Concrete Questions: Can you think of a time when grammar confusion led to misunderstanding? What grammar rules do you find most challenging?

2. Abstract Questions: Does grammar reflect intelligence? Can breaking grammar rules ever be justified?

b. Punctuation: The use of symbols (e.g., commas, periods, question marks) in writing to separate sentences and clarify meaning.

1. Concrete Questions: How does misplaced punctuation change the meaning of a sentence? What punctuation rules do you often forget?

2. Abstract Questions: Is punctuation a form of artistic expression? Can punctuation influence a readerâ€™s tone and interpretation?

c. Spelling: The correct arrangement of letters to form words. Proper spelling helps maintain credibility and readability in writing.

1. Concrete Questions: What words do you often misspell? How do spelling errors affect communication?

2. Abstract Questions: Does spelling proficiency reflect education level? With autocorrect available, does spelling still matter?

3. Activity

a. Review examples from the course material:

1. Incorrect vs. correct usage of common grammatical errors (Canvas module: Week 1 | Common Mistakes Made with Grammar, Punctuation, and Spelling).

2. Peer discussion: Share experiences of how grammar mistakes have affected communication.

4. Supplementary Resources

a. Grammarlyâ€™s Guide to Grammar

b. Purdue OWL Grammar Resources



IV. Assignment Overview: Narrative Essay (30 minutes)

1. Purpose of the Narrative Essay

a. Students will craft a personal story that conveys a lesson learned or an important moment. The assignment is scaffolded to begin with prewriting and idea generation.

2. Key Concept - Prewriting

a. The initial stage of the writing process that involves brainstorming, outlining, and organizing ideas before drafting.

1. Concrete Questions: What methods have you used to organize ideas before writing? What challenges do you face during prewriting?

2. Abstract Questions: Is prewriting necessary for creativity? Can too much planning hinder spontaneous ideas?

3. Structure Breakdown

a. Prewriting Stage (Week 1): Brainstorming ideas, outlining, and drafting.

b. Key Components to Focus on:

1. Selecting a meaningful personal story.

2. Developing a clear beginning, middle, and end.

3. Incorporating vivid descriptions and dialogue.

4. Rubric Highlights

a. Clear thesis or moral of the story (10 points).

b. Three supporting points and detailed development (10 points).

c. Grammar, spelling, and punctuation accuracy (10 points).

5. Discussion Questions

a. What makes a personal story engaging for readers?

b. How do you choose which life events are worth writing about?



V. Weekly Writing Warm-Up Overview (20 minutes)

1. Objective

a. Encourage students to write regularly without self-judgment to improve fluency and comfort with the writing process.

2. Prompt for the Week

a. What does writing mean to you? This serves as both an icebreaker and an introduction to personal expression in writing.

3. Grading Criteria

a. Full credit: Submission meets length and prompt requirements.

b. No credit: Insufficient effort or incomplete submission.



VI. Independent Work Time (35 minutes)

1. Tasks for Today

a. Read through Week 1 Overview and Learning Outcomes in Canvas.

b. Begin brainstorming for Narrative Essay prewriting assignment.

c. Complete reading:

1. The Writing Process (Canvas > Week 1 Lectures).

2. How to Prewrite for Your Assignment (Canvas > Week 1 Lectures).

2. Instructor Role

a. Walk around and offer guidance.

b. Answer questions about grammar concepts and the narrative essay.

c. Provide one-on-one feedback on warm-up responses.



VII. Closing Discussion (5 minutes)

1. Reflection Questions

a. What was your biggest takeaway from todayâ€™s session?

b. What do you feel most confident about moving forward in the course?

c. What are your goals for improving your writing?

2. Reminders for Tomorrow

a. Review assigned readings on audience and purpose in writing.

b. Be ready to discuss â€œWhat does it mean to write with purpose?â€

c. Continue working on your prewriting assignment for the Narrative Essay.



VIII. Summary of Day 1 Learning Goals

1. Understand the importance of grammar, punctuation, and spelling in writing.

2. Gain familiarity with the Narrative Essay assignment structure.

3. Begin developing personal writing habits through daily warm-ups.

4. Engage in collaborative discussion and self-directed learning.

This format should be easy to copy and paste into a Word document without requiring adjustments

Week 3 | Summary

Week 3 | Overview
Drafting and Revision
Now that you've gone through the prewriting, brainstorming, and initial drafting stages, it's time to polish your paper. In this module, we'll equip you with strategies to revise your writing, ensuring it communicates your ideas effectively. 

"Revision is the key to successful writing." (Hofmann, 2017, p. 351)

Our focus will be on the difference between revision and editing. While editing focuses on grammar, spelling, punctuation, and other surface-level corrections, revision is focused on the larger picture. The goal is to ensure your content, structure, and overall message align with your intended purpose and engage your audience effectively. 

Revision can be challenging but is a crucial phase of good writing. This is your chance to take a step back and critically evaluate your work. You will learn to identify areas that need improvement, such as strengthening your arguments, refining your organization, improving the flow of ideas, and ensuring coherence throughout your writing. As a result, you will enhance the impact of your writing on your readers by giving it a makeover. 

This week, we will help you gain a practical understanding of how to approach the revision process. You will learn how to be an objective reader of your work and learn what works well and what needs improvement. We'll also discuss techniques for incorporating feedback from peers or instructors, which can offer new perspectives and further enhance your writing. 

So, get ready to begin your final drafting and revision. By honing these skills, you'll take your writing to the next level, crafting compelling and polished pieces that leave a lasting impression.  

  TIP
Put your draft aside for a little while. Time away from your essay will allow for a more objective evaluation. When you return to the draft, be honest about your thoughts on the paper.

Reference
Hofmann, A. H. (2017). Scientific writing and communication: Papers, proposals, and presentations (3rd ed.). Oxford University Press.

Week 3 | Learning Outcomes
Learning Outcomes
Learning Outcomes
Upon successful completion of this module, you will be able to:

CO1: Use proper grammar, punctuation, and spelling.
CO2: Identify various writing techniques, including audience analysis, diction, author purpose, genre, and conventions when reading given texts.
CO3: Recognize essay types such as personal narrative, process analysis, comparison and contrast, and persuasion essays. 
CO4: Explain and demonstrate steps in the writing process.
CO5: Incorporate valid and appropriate outside sources in original texts.    
CO6: Compose essays that are mode, genre, and audience-appropriate, well-developed, mechanically correct, and communicate clearly with the audience.
Week 3 Learning Outcomes
You will demonstrate understanding through the ability to:

Identify parts of speech, proper capitalization, and end punctuation. (CO1)
Identify proper sentence structure, subject, and predicate. (CO1)
Differentiate a complete sentence from a fragment, run-on, and comma splice. (CO1)
Write well-developed, well-organized paragraphs. (CO1)
Identify agreement and tenses in parts of speech. (CO1)
Demonstrate an understanding of appropriate diction through discussion board exercises. (CO1)
Recognize dangling modifiers and punctuation such as apostrophes, hyphens, question marks and their proper usage. (CO1)
Analyze and correct grammatical and mechanical errors in a given passage. (CO1)
Identify commonly confused words, spellings, and proper abbreviations. (CO1)
Write a well-developed, well organized paragraph. (CO2)
Express an understanding of narrative essay writing. (CO3)
Demonstrate the characteristics and organization of narrative writing. (CO3)
Explain the characteristics of academic writing. (CO3)
Classify different styles of writing, such as personal narratives and compare/contrast, based on their components and structure. (CO3)
Demonstrate an understanding of revision and drafting. (CO4)
Demonstrate an understanding of valid and appropriate outside sources. (CO5)
Identify the basic rules of APA formatting. (CO5)
Discuss and perform a peer review. (CO5)
Identify types of outside sources and the reason to use them. (CO5)
Compose a personal narrative essay. (CO6)

Week 3 | Writing the Body and Conclusion of a Paper
Writing the Body and Conclusion of a Paper
Moving on in our lectures about writing a narrative essay, this week, we will focus on the body and conclusion of the paper. When writing the body of a paper, it is important to create organized and well-defined paragraphs outlining chronological events and details describing those events. Each paragraph should concentrate on one concept or idea. Your body paragraphs should present enough information to describe the event so your reader can understand and connect the story's details.

Your conclusion is the final paragraph when you finish your paper's body. This section is the concluding part of your story or argument. It is your last chance to leave a solid impression on your audience. Instead of restating your thesis or essential points, reinforce the ideas you've already made, making sure your audience sees the value of what you've stated. You can also discuss the broader implications of your story or paper. Your conclusion can be the crowning glory of your well-written paper.  

5 paragraph hamburger essay: Introduction=bun, 1st point=lettuce, 2nd point=tomato, 3rd point=meat, conclusion=bun

Week 3 | Organizing Your Essay
Organizing Your Essay
To elevate your paper's impact on the audience, it is important first to organize and connect details by time (chronologically) or emphasis (importance). Doing this will make your argument more cohesive and highlight the most important points you wish to make. 



For instance, in your narrative paper, if you were writing about when your family moved from one state to another, you should begin by discussing the major events leading up to the move. Chronologically, this would happen three months before, one month before, one week early, etc. On the other hand, you could emphasize important events that precipitated the move, regardless of the timeline. For example, your neighborhood changed, and your parents were unhappy with their careers. You started school, your mom started volunteering, your dad started looking for a new job; his friend in another state sent him an interesting job posting, and then he applied for the job, was offered the job, and accepted it. The timeline itself didn't matter, but the important events did.  

Remember to be clear and concise in your writing, regardless of your approach. A well-organized paper will engage readers and help them follow your line of thought. Ultimately, this will make for a more organized and effective paper.  

After you finish this page, click Next to continue to Transitions and Transitional Phrases.

Week 3 | Transitions and Transitional Phrases
Transitions and Transitional Phrases
The body of your paper is where you develop your ideas and provide evidence to support your claims. To keep your paper well-organized and easy to follow, it is important to use transitions between paragraphs and sentences. Transitions help create a flow so the reader can easily follow your thoughts. 

Many different types of transitions can be used in the body of your paper. Some common transitional phrases include:

In addition
Furthermore
Similarly
Moreover
However
On the other hand
Review Additional Transition Words Below
EMPAHSIS

CONTRAST

At this time
Previously
First/Firstly
Second/Secondly
Third/Thirdly
Finally
Subsequently
Above all
Before
Last, but not least
First and foremost
Each type of transition serves a different purpose and can be used in different ways. For example, use a transitional phrase at the beginning of a paragraph to introduce a new idea or at the end to conclude an idea. Use a transition within a sentence to contrast two ideas or to emphasize a point. Experiment with transitions and transitional phrases to find what works best for you and your argument. 
 
In your conclusion section, transitions can serve a unique purpose to indicate your final or closing thoughts. Phrases such as "In conclusion," "To sum up," or "Finally" will signal to the reader that you are coming to the end of your paper and help them remember the main points of your paper.

Watch the video below to learn more about transitions and phrases!


Week 3 | Common Methods of Conclusions
Common Methods of Conclusions
There are a few common methods for concluding a paper that can help elevate its impact. First, summarize the main points of the paper. This would include a brief recap of what was discussed in the body of the paper and how it all ties together. Another option for concluding a paper is to discuss the broader implications or lessons learned while researching your paper. You could also make a call to action urging readers to act based on what they have read. Whatever method is used, ensuring the conclusion is well-structured and flows smoothly is important.

conclusion options: to review, in closing, in the end, on the whole, etc.

Long Description

Week 3 | Incorporating Feedback
Incorporating Feedback
When you revise your essay, you examine it as an entire product. It’s important to analyze the full effect of the essay. When you do this, you review the essay for unity, support, and coherence. Feedback from your instructor or another trusted source will help you focus your efforts in the revision process. While not all pieces of feedback must be explicitly followed, it is important to consider all feedback as important. When trusted sources give you feedback on your essay’s unity, support, coherence, or sentence structures, note and revise those portions! 


A word document with a comment of "Should there be a citation here?" referencing to "dimension-word" in the paragraph.

After you finish this page, click Next to continue to Specifics of Feedback: Coherence.

Week 3 | Specifics of Feedback: Coherence
Coherence


When revising an essay, paying attention to several key elements is crucial. This includes unity, support, coherence, and close sentence skills. When it comes to unity, ensure that each paragraph and sentence contributes to the central theme or thesis statement. Remove any irrelevant or repetitive information that detracts from the overall focus. This is also true of the supporting details provided to support your story. Ensure that each point is supported with relevant and clear details and examples and clearly show a sequence of events. Each point should relate to the central theme or thesis statement. Strengthen weak details and eliminate any irrelevant events that don't relate to the story. For example, a briefly summarized important scene in the story could be enhanced with dialogue and sensory description.

Coherence is being logical and consistent. It is essential for the overall flow of your essay. Review the progression of ideas between paragraphs and sentences, ensuring effective use of transitions. Ideas should connect logically and smoothly. This guides the reader from one point to the next. Consider using transitional words and phrases to enhance coherence. Lastly, attention to sentence skills, including grammar, punctuation, and clarity. Review sentence structure to ensure that it is varied and well-crafted. Check for grammatical errors. This includes subject-verb agreement, verb tense consistency, and proper word usage. Additionally, replace any awkward or unclear sentences with concise and precise language.

After you finish this page, click Next to continue to Specifics of Feedback: Grammar.

Week 3 | Specifics of Feedback: Grammar
Grammar
If you're like most people, grammar isn't your favorite subject. It can be confusing and frustrating, especially when writing a flawless essay. But don't despair! You can use some simple techniques to ensure your essay is error-free. 
 
Other than proofreading your work, ask someone else to read your essay. A fresh set of eyes can often spot errors that you've missed. Ask a friend or family member to read through your work and give their honest feedback. 

Have a reliable source you can turn to for grammar questions or concerns. Grammarly.comLinks to an external site. is a free website that offers everything from basic grammar concepts to more advanced function issues.

Watch the videos below to learn about common grammatical mistakes: capitalization, commas, and apostrophes.

Week 3 | Weekly Writing Warm-Up
   
Weekly Writing Warm-Up 
 Introduction
Due by the end of the week.

Welcome to the Weekly Writing Warm-Up, where you will practice the art of writing. For many of us, the extent of our writing happens in text over our phones, so writing papers and essays feels awkward. To this end, we will participate in Weekly Writing Warm-Ups. These warm-ups are less about what you write and more about putting your fingers on your computer keys. You will not be critiqued on your paragraph or paper structure, but the content does need to be clear. Consistent writing can be a valuable tool to enhance your communication skills, creativity, and self-expression. Each week, you will be given new instructions (see below) to reflect on and write about. This warm-up activity does not require prework, outlines, or drafts.

Here are some great guidelines when you are starting a free-write warm-up exercise:

Keep your fingers moving on the keyboard for 5 minutes. 
Write everything that comes to mind, even seemingly unrelated comments like, "I hate writing. This is too hard. I don't think I would like eating squid if I had the chance."
Don't self-edit. Get it all down without worrying about how it looks or whether or not it is correct.
Be outrageous. Use vocabulary and descriptions that sound overboard, silly, or absurd. Make comparisons and connections to other subjects (even if they seem at first glance to be irrelevant or unrelated).
Keep writing, no matter what, until your first 5 minutes is up, and then stop.
Instructions
Perform the following tasks:

On a Word document or the text entry box:

Complete this week's prompt:
Go to any credible news outlet and read an article about a current news story. Think about the writing critically. First, free-write about the story you read. What was it about? What are your thoughts and opinions about it? Second, if you were to revise that story, what would you consider adding to the story or removing? Is it missing something critical, or does it veer off course somewhere? 

Read what you wrote for the Week 2 Warm-Up assignment. Was it interesting? Does it sound like your voice? Could it have been better? Write your thoughts in 250 words on what you wrote.

Save your completed Week 3 Weekly Writing Warm-Up using the proper naming convention.
Estimated Time for Completion
The estimated time to complete this activity is 5 minutes.

Completion
The naming convention is ENG101_wk#_warm_up_firstintiallastname_date—for example, ENG101_wk3_warm_up_jsmith_mmddyyyy.

Week 3 | Discussion: Narrative Essay - First Draft
Narrative Essay - First Draft
 Instructions
Your discussion will occur in class.

Discussion Topic
Read your classmates' narrative essays and provide constructive feedback to two classmates.   

Address the following in your peer reviews:

Discuss the flow and organization of the paragraphs and explain suggestions for any revisions/reordering to the paragraphs.
Comment on the strength of the thesis statement and how it flows into the body of the essay or why the thesis needs revision.
Explain how their essays are well-edited and provide additional editing suggestions for them.

If you are not sure how to provide constructive feedback, address the following question prompts:

What is one thing the author did effectively (could be a strength in grammar or technique)? What is one thing the author can improve?
Can you identify your peer's thesis statement? Is it effective? Can it be improved? If so, how?
Which pre-writing strategies do you think the author can utilize or further utilize to improve their paragraph and why?
Regarding writing techniques, how did the author demonstrate consideration of audience, diction, and/or purpose?

 Introduction
Due by the end of the week.

In your Week 3 Assignment, you will be asked to submit the final draft of your narrative essay using APA formatting. Your essay should be a minimum of five paragraphs, from 500-800 words, and, at most, four pages, not including your title page. You should have an opening, narrative paragraphs (the body of your essay), and a conclusion. Be sure you have taken the time to incorporate the feedback from your peers and instructor, paying close attention to concepts such as unity, support, coherence, sentence structure, and grammar. As a reminder, APA formatting is part of the rubric for the final essay, and your grade will be based, in part, on your diligence in using APA.

Introduction with thesis statement; body to support sub-points; conclusion restating the thesis.
Integrated proper use of all grammar concepts covered throughout the course.

Consists of 500 to 800 words in length (five paragraphs)

Week 3 | Summary
Summary
This week, we reached a pivotal part of our writing journey. After learning about prewriting, brainstorming, and initial drafting phases, it was time to refine and elevate our papers through strategic revision. We focused on distinguishing between revision and editing, highlighting that editing addresses surface-level corrections like grammar, spelling, and punctuation. In contrast, revision encompasses a broader view of the writing, ensuring alignment with the intended purpose and effective audience engagement.

We now know that revision is a fundamental aspect of exceptional writing. This phase provided an opportunity to step back, critically assess our work, and identify areas for improvement—be it strengthening arguments, refining organization, enhancing idea flow, or ensuring overall coherence. Through this process, we aimed to elevate our writing.

You learned practical strategies for the revision process, becoming objective readers of your work. We also discussed techniques for effectively incorporating feedback from peers or instructors.

In alignment with our course's learning objectives, we prioritized proper grammar, punctuation, and spelling (CO1). Moreover, we emphasized the importance of identifying various writing techniques, including audience analysis, diction, author purpose, genre, and conventions when reading given texts (CO2). We learned about different essay types, focusing on the narrative (CO3). Additionally, we explained and demonstrated the steps in the writing process (CO4) and emphasized incorporating valid and appropriate outside sources in our original texts (CO5). You're on the path to elevating your writing by honing these critical skills!

Week 3 | Summary

Week 3 | Overview
Drafting and Revision
Now that you've gone through the prewriting, brainstorming, and initial drafting stages, it's time to polish your paper. In this module, we'll equip you with strategies to revise your writing, ensuring it communicates your ideas effectively. 

"Revision is the key to successful writing." (Hofmann, 2017, p. 351)

Our focus will be on the difference between revision and editing. While editing focuses on grammar, spelling, punctuation, and other surface-level corrections, revision is focused on the larger picture. The goal is to ensure your content, structure, and overall message align with your intended purpose and engage your audience effectively. 

Revision can be challenging but is a crucial phase of good writing. This is your chance to take a step back and critically evaluate your work. You will learn to identify areas that need improvement, such as strengthening your arguments, refining your organization, improving the flow of ideas, and ensuring coherence throughout your writing. As a result, you will enhance the impact of your writing on your readers by giving it a makeover. 

This week, we will help you gain a practical understanding of how to approach the revision process. You will learn how to be an objective reader of your work and learn what works well and what needs improvement. We'll also discuss techniques for incorporating feedback from peers or instructors, which can offer new perspectives and further enhance your writing. 

So, get ready to begin your final drafting and revision. By honing these skills, you'll take your writing to the next level, crafting compelling and polished pieces that leave a lasting impression.  

  TIP
Put your draft aside for a little while. Time away from your essay will allow for a more objective evaluation. When you return to the draft, be honest about your thoughts on the paper.

Reference
Hofmann, A. H. (2017). Scientific writing and communication: Papers, proposals, and presentations (3rd ed.). Oxford University Press.

Week 3 | Learning Outcomes
Learning Outcomes
Learning Outcomes
Upon successful completion of this module, you will be able to:

CO1: Use proper grammar, punctuation, and spelling.
CO2: Identify various writing techniques, including audience analysis, diction, author purpose, genre, and conventions when reading given texts.
CO3: Recognize essay types such as personal narrative, process analysis, comparison and contrast, and persuasion essays. 
CO4: Explain and demonstrate steps in the writing process.
CO5: Incorporate valid and appropriate outside sources in original texts.    
CO6: Compose essays that are mode, genre, and audience-appropriate, well-developed, mechanically correct, and communicate clearly with the audience.

Week 3 | Writing the Body and Conclusion of a Paper
Writing the Body and Conclusion of a Paper
Moving on in our lectures about writing a narrative essay, this week, we will focus on the body and conclusion of the paper. When writing the body of a paper, it is important to create organized and well-defined paragraphs outlining chronological events and details describing those events. Each paragraph should concentrate on one concept or idea. Your body paragraphs should present enough information to describe the event so your reader can understand and connect the story's details.

Your conclusion is the final paragraph when you finish your paper's body. This section is the concluding part of your story or argument. It is your last chance to leave a solid impression on your audience. Instead of restating your thesis or essential points, reinforce the ideas you've already made, making sure your audience sees the value of what you've stated. You can also discuss the broader implications of your story or paper. Your conclusion can be the crowning glory of your well-written paper.  

5 paragraph hamburger essay: Introduction=bun, 1st point=lettuce, 2nd point=tomato, 3rd point=meat, conclusion=bun

Week 3 | Organizing Your Essay
Organizing Your Essay
To elevate your paper's impact on the audience, it is important first to organize and connect details by time (chronologically) or emphasis (importance). Doing this will make your argument more cohesive and highlight the most important points you wish to make. 



For instance, in your narrative paper, if you were writing about when your family moved from one state to another, you should begin by discussing the major events leading up to the move. Chronologically, this would happen three months before, one month before, one week early, etc. On the other hand, you could emphasize important events that precipitated the move, regardless of the timeline. For example, your neighborhood changed, and your parents were unhappy with their careers. You started school, your mom started volunteering, your dad started looking for a new job; his friend in another state sent him an interesting job posting, and then he applied for the job, was offered the job, and accepted it. The timeline itself didn't matter, but the important events did.  

Remember to be clear and concise in your writing, regardless of your approach. A well-organized paper will engage readers and help them follow your line of thought. Ultimately, this will make for a more organized and effective paper.  

After you finish this page, click Next to continue to Transitions and Transitional Phrases.

Week 3 | Transitions and Transitional Phrases
Transitions and Transitional Phrases
The body of your paper is where you develop your ideas and provide evidence to support your claims. To keep your paper well-organized and easy to follow, it is important to use transitions between paragraphs and sentences. Transitions help create a flow so the reader can easily follow your thoughts. 

Many different types of transitions can be used in the body of your paper. Some common transitional phrases include:

In addition
Furthermore
Similarly
Moreover
However
On the other hand
Review Additional Transition Words Below
EMPAHSIS
ADDITION
CONTRAST
ORDER
Undoubtedly
Unquestionably
Obviously
Particularly/In particular
Especially
Clearly
Importantly
Absolutely
Definitely
Without a doubt
Indeed
It should be noted

Along with
Apart from this
Moreover
Furthermore
Also
Too
As well as that
Besides
In addition not only ... but also
In addition to this
Additionally/In additional	
Unlike
Nevertheless
On the other hand
Nonetheless
Despite/In spite of
In contrast (to)
Contrary to
Whereas
Alternatively
Conversely
Even so
Differing from

Following
At this time
Previously
First/Firstly
Second/Secondly
Third/Thirdly
Finally
Subsequently
Above all
Before
Last, but not least
First and foremost
Each type of transition serves a different purpose and can be used in different ways. For example, use a transitional phrase at the beginning of a paragraph to introduce a new idea or at the end to conclude an idea. Use a transition within a sentence to contrast two ideas or to emphasize a point. Experiment with transitions and transitional phrases to find what works best for you and your argument. 
 
In your conclusion section, transitions can serve a unique purpose to indicate your final or closing thoughts. Phrases such as "In conclusion," "To sum up," or "Finally" will signal to the reader that you are coming to the end of your paper and help them remember the main points of your paper.

Watch the video below to learn more about transitions and phrases!


Week 3 | Common Methods of Conclusions
Common Methods of Conclusions
There are a few common methods for concluding a paper that can help elevate its impact. First, summarize the main points of the paper. This would include a brief recap of what was discussed in the body of the paper and how it all ties together. Another option for concluding a paper is to discuss the broader implications or lessons learned while researching your paper. You could also make a call to action urging readers to act based on what they have read. Whatever method is used, ensuring the conclusion is well-structured and flows smoothly is important.

conclusion options: to review, in closing, in the end, on the whole, etc.

Long Description


Watch the video below to learn how to write a conclusion!

Week 3 | Incorporating Feedback
Incorporating Feedback
When you revise your essay, you examine it as an entire product. It’s important to analyze the full effect of the essay. When you do this, you review the essay for unity, support, and coherence. Feedback from your instructor or another trusted source will help you focus your efforts in the revision process. While not all pieces of feedback must be explicitly followed, it is important to consider all feedback as important. When trusted sources give you feedback on your essay’s unity, support, coherence, or sentence structures, note and revise those portions! 


A word document with a comment of "Should there be a citation here?" referencing to "dimension-word" in the paragraph.

After you finish this page, click Next to continue to Specifics of Feedback: Coherence.

Week 3 | Specifics of Feedback: Coherence
Coherence


When revising an essay, paying attention to several key elements is crucial. This includes unity, support, coherence, and close sentence skills. When it comes to unity, ensure that each paragraph and sentence contributes to the central theme or thesis statement. Remove any irrelevant or repetitive information that detracts from the overall focus. This is also true of the supporting details provided to support your story. Ensure that each point is supported with relevant and clear details and examples and clearly show a sequence of events. Each point should relate to the central theme or thesis statement. Strengthen weak details and eliminate any irrelevant events that don't relate to the story. For example, a briefly summarized important scene in the story could be enhanced with dialogue and sensory description.

Coherence is being logical and consistent. It is essential for the overall flow of your essay. Review the progression of ideas between paragraphs and sentences, ensuring effective use of transitions. Ideas should connect logically and smoothly. This guides the reader from one point to the next. Consider using transitional words and phrases to enhance coherence. Lastly, attention to sentence skills, including grammar, punctuation, and clarity. Review sentence structure to ensure that it is varied and well-crafted. Check for grammatical errors. This includes subject-verb agreement, verb tense consistency, and proper word usage. Additionally, replace any awkward or unclear sentences with concise and precise language.

After you finish this page, click Next to continue to Specifics of Feedback: Grammar.

Week 3 | Specifics of Feedback: Grammar
Grammar
If you're like most people, grammar isn't your favorite subject. It can be confusing and frustrating, especially when writing a flawless essay. But don't despair! You can use some simple techniques to ensure your essay is error-free. 
 
Other than proofreading your work, ask someone else to read your essay. A fresh set of eyes can often spot errors that you've missed. Ask a friend or family member to read through your work and give their honest feedback. 

Have a reliable source you can turn to for grammar questions or concerns. Grammarly.comLinks to an external site. is a free website that offers everything from basic grammar concepts to more advanced function issues.

Watch the videos below to learn about common grammatical mistakes: capitalization, commas, and apostrophes.

Common Grammar Rules

Week 3 | Weekly Writing Warm-Up
   
Weekly Writing Warm-Up 
 Introduction
Due by the end of the week.

Welcome to the Weekly Writing Warm-Up, where you will practice the art of writing. For many of us, the extent of our writing happens in text over our phones, so writing papers and essays feels awkward. To this end, we will participate in Weekly Writing Warm-Ups. These warm-ups are less about what you write and more about putting your fingers on your computer keys. You will not be critiqued on your paragraph or paper structure, but the content does need to be clear. Consistent writing can be a valuable tool to enhance your communication skills, creativity, and self-expression. Each week, you will be given new instructions (see below) to reflect on and write about. This warm-up activity does not require prework, outlines, or drafts.

Here are some great guidelines when you are starting a free-write warm-up exercise:

Keep your fingers moving on the keyboard for 5 minutes. 
Write everything that comes to mind, even seemingly unrelated comments like, "I hate writing. This is too hard. I don't think I would like eating squid if I had the chance."
Don't self-edit. Get it all down without worrying about how it looks or whether or not it is correct.
Be outrageous. Use vocabulary and descriptions that sound overboard, silly, or absurd. Make comparisons and connections to other subjects (even if they seem at first glance to be irrelevant or unrelated).
Keep writing, no matter what, until your first 5 minutes is up, and then stop.
Instructions
Perform the following tasks:

On a Word document or the text entry box:

Complete this week's prompt:
Go to any credible news outlet and read an article about a current news story. Think about the writing critically. First, free-write about the story you read. What was it about? What are your thoughts and opinions about it? Second, if you were to revise that story, what would you consider adding to the story or removing? Is it missing something critical, or does it veer off course somewhere? 

Read what you wrote for the Week 2 Warm-Up assignment. Was it interesting? Does it sound like your voice? Could it have been better? Write your thoughts in 250 words on what you wrote.

Save your completed Week 3 Weekly Writing Warm-Up using the proper naming convention.

The estimated time to complete this activity is 5 minutes.

Week 3 | Discussion: Narrative Essay - First Draft
Narrative Essay - First Draft
 Instructions
Your discussion will occur in class.

Discussion Topic
Read your classmates' narrative essays and provide constructive feedback to two classmates.   

Address the following in your peer reviews:

Discuss the flow and organization of the paragraphs and explain suggestions for any revisions/reordering to the paragraphs.
Comment on the strength of the thesis statement and how it flows into the body of the essay or why the thesis needs revision.
Explain how their essays are well-edited and provide additional editing suggestions for them.

If you are not sure how to provide constructive feedback, address the following question prompts:

What is one thing the author did effectively (could be a strength in grammar or technique)? What is one thing the author can improve?
Can you identify your peer's thesis statement? Is it effective? Can it be improved? If so, how?
Which pre-writing strategies do you think the author can utilize or further utilize to improve their paragraph and why?
Regarding writing techniques, how did the author demonstrate consideration of audience, diction, and/or purpose?

Week 3 | Assignment: Narrative Essay - Final Draft
   
Narrative Essay Final Draft Assignment
 Introduction
Due by the end of the week.

In your Week 3 Assignment, you will be asked to submit the final draft of your narrative essay using APA formatting. Your essay should be a minimum of five paragraphs, from 500-800 words, and, at most, four pages, not including your title page. You should have an opening, narrative paragraphs (the body of your essay), and a conclusion. Be sure you have taken the time to incorporate the feedback from your peers and instructor, paying close attention to concepts such as unity, support, coherence, sentence structure, and grammar. As a reminder, APA formatting is part of the rubric for the final essay, and your grade will be based, in part, on your diligence in using APA.

Instructions
Perform the following tasks:

Complete the reading assignment and the interactive lecture before attempting this assignment.
To complete this assignment:
Revise your essay using the feedback you have received from your peer, and your instructor.
Use the assignment details document below to write your revised essay.
Proofread and edit your essay carefully, following the rubric below.
Use the following criteria as a checklist for your final essay:
Title page
Introduction with thesis statement; body to support sub-points; conclusion restating the thesis.
Integrated proper use of all grammar concepts covered throughout the course.
Use Times New Roman, 12 pt. font; 1-inch margins; double-spaced.
Consists of 500 to 800 words in length (five paragraphs)
Use the plagiarism tool to check your summary against unintended plagiarism.
Refer to the guidelines below to complete each phase of your essay following the required APA format:
APA (7th Edition) Student Sample Paper
APA Formatting and Style Guide (7th Edition)
Spell-check your essay.
Save your work using the proper naming convention
Estimated Time for Completion
The estimated time to complete this activity is 3 - 4 hours.

Completion
Use the proper naming convention when saving your assignment. The naming convention is ENG101_wk3_assn_firstintiallastname_date—for example, ENG101_wk3_assn_jsmith_mmddyyyy.
Submit the Week 3 Assignment by the end of the week. See the video below to review the process.

Week 3 Assignment
Criteria	Ratings	Pts
This criterion is linked to a Learning OutcomeFinal Draft:
Organization
30 to >27.0 pts
Exemplary
Strong topic sentence addresses assignment prompt in highly focused and inviting way. Reasons, details, facts relate to prompt in compelling, highly effective ways. Varied and subtle transitions enhance the flow of the piece. Strong conclusion revisits main idea with imagination. Thorough plan with useful detail (when required)
27 to >22.0 pts
Accomplished
Topic sentence clearly focuses on prompt. Reasons, details, facts directly relate to prompt. Effective transitions; Interesting conclusion summarizes main idea. Clear, careful plan (when required)
22 to >18.0 pts
Developing
Attempts a topic sentence. Reasons, details, facts mostly relate to prompt. Ineffective transitions. Weak, repetitive conclusion. Sketchy, inaccurate plan (when required).
18 to >0 pts
Beginning
Absent/unclear topic sentence. Reasons, details, facts do not relate to prompt. No transitions. No or inappropriate conclusion. Little/ no plan (when required)
30 pts
This criterion is linked to a Learning OutcomeFinal Draft:
Content
30 to >27.0 pts
Exemplary
High quality information focuses on the prompt in engaging ways. Intriguing and/or compelling examples, evidence and explanations bring topic to life. Prompt/task fully developed
27 to >22.0 pts
Accomplished
All information is accurate and focused on task/topic. Examples/explanations support topic. Prompt/task developed consistently
22 to >18.0 pts
Developing
Information sometimes repeated and/or wanders from prompt/task; may contain inaccuracies. Examples/explanations are incomplete; lacks detail. Prompt/task/topic inconsistently or not adequately developed
18 to >0 pts
Beginning
Information inaccurate, inadequate or omitted. Examples/explanations unclear or missing. Prompt/task not developed or may not be address
30 pts
This criterion is linked to a Learning OutcomeFinal Draft:
Style
20 to >18.0 pts
Exemplary
Rich, accurate vocabulary. Engaging tone is appropriate for audience. Varied sentence structure enhances the meaning and flow of the writing when read silently and/or aloud
18 to >15.0 pts
Accomplished
Clear, accurate vocabulary. Tone is appropriate for audience. Complete sentences with some variety allow the writing to competently flow when read silently and/or aloud
15 to >12.0 pts
Developing
Basic or inaccurate vocabulary. Tone is inappropriate or immature. Awkward, simple, or incomplete sentences with little variety detract from the flow of the writing when read silently and/or aloud
12 to >0 pts
Beginning
Inaccurate vocabulary. Tone misleads audience. Awkward, short, choppy sentences, run-ons, and/or fragments make clear understanding of the writing nearly impossible when read silently and/or aloud
20 pts
This criterion is linked to a Learning OutcomeFinal Draft:
Writing Conventions and APA formatting
20 to >18.0 pts
Exemplary
Accurate spelling, grammar, and punctuation (0 errors). Accurate Use of APA formatting consistently throughout the paper, title and reference page, with attention to in-text citations and reference entry page (no to very few errors). Manipulates conventions for stylistic effect—and it works!
18 to >15.0 pts
Accomplished
Grammatical, spelling & punctuation errors are rare (2 errors per page avg.). Exhibits APA formatting throughout the paper, however, layout contains a few minor errors that do not detract from the overall paper.
15 to >12.0 pts
Developing
Grammatical, spelling & punctuation errors are common (3 errors per page avg.). Exhibits a basic knowledge of APA formatting throughout the paper, but does not meet many APA requirements. Several APA errors and In-text citations OR reference entry are missing which detracts from the overall paper.
12 to >0 pts
Beginning
Grammatical, spelling & punctuation errors significantly detract from meaning of paper (4+ errors per page avg.). Fails to exhibit basic knowledge of APA style; no reference entry or in-text citations.

Week 3 | Summary
Summary
This week, we reached a pivotal part of our writing journey. After learning about prewriting, brainstorming, and initial drafting phases, it was time to refine and elevate our papers through strategic revision. We focused on distinguishing between revision and editing, highlighting that editing addresses surface-level corrections like grammar, spelling, and punctuation. In contrast, revision encompasses a broader view of the writing, ensuring alignment with the intended purpose and effective audience engagement.

We now know that revision is a fundamental aspect of exceptional writing. This phase provided an opportunity to step back, critically assess our work, and identify areas for improvement—be it strengthening arguments, refining organization, enhancing idea flow, or ensuring overall coherence. Through this process, we aimed to elevate our writing.

You learned practical strategies for the revision process, becoming objective readers of your work. We also discussed techniques for effectively incorporating feedback from peers or instructors.

In alignment with our course's learning objectives, we prioritized proper grammar, punctuation, and spelling (CO1). Moreover, we emphasized the importance of identifying various writing techniques, including audience analysis, diction, author purpose, genre, and conventions when reading given texts (CO2). We learned about different essay types, focusing on the narrative (CO3). Additionally, we explained and demonstrated the steps in the writing process (CO4) and emphasized incorporating valid and appropriate outside sources in our original texts (CO5). You're on the path to elevating your writing by honing these critical skills!

Congratulations! You completed the third week of the course! Click Next to read the Week 4 Overview.

```
i've attached the most recent version (v4) and the previous version (3);

below I've pasted the 🛠 RULES, PROCESSES, & PROTOCOLS FOR CLASS SCHEDULES*;

Below is a **clear, detailed framework** we will use to develop **all future class schedules.**  

---

# **🛠 RULES, PROCESSES, & PROTOCOLS FOR CLASS SCHEDULES**  
*(To be applied to all future weeks, including Week 5)*  

---

## **📌 1. STRUCTURAL FRAMEWORK (Macro-Level)**
Every class schedule follows a **consistent scaffolding** where:  

✅ **Each section builds on the last (micro-level: section-to-section).**  
✅ **Each day connects and escalates concepts (daily structure).**  
✅ **Each week builds logically upon the last (macro-level: week-to-week).**  
✅ **The entire course moves toward a larger goal (meta-level: semester-long cohesion).**  
✅ **Every transition ties together past discussions, present objectives, and future learning.**  

---

## **📌 2. ORDER & CONTENT OF EACH CLASS SCHEDULE**
Each week follows **this specific order and organization:**  

### **🔹 1. Thematic Introduction & Warm-Up Discussion**  
- Begins with an **engaging theme or central idea** that will run through the class.  
- **Pet Peeve / Complaining / Purging Question** → Related to **the week’s theme** but explored from an unexpected, creative perspective.  
- **Non-Literary, Therapy-Like Warm-Up Questions** → Designed to **open up students** and get them thinking critically.  
- **Reflection on the Previous Week** → Using **Concrete, Abstract, Connecting, and Exploratory** questions to bridge themes.  
- **Establishing Core Questions for the Week** → What overarching literary and philosophical questions will drive discussions?  

### **🔹 2. Weekly Overview & Objectives**  
- Clearly outlines **what students will learn and explore** throughout the week.  
- **Objectives focus on analysis, literary techniques, and broader themes.**  
- Provides **a roadmap** so students know what to expect.  

### **🔹 3. Suggested Weekly Pacing & Checklist**  
- 📅 **Recommended Reading & Assignment Schedule** → Helps students pace themselves.  
- ✅ **Checklist of Readings, Assignments, & Supplementary Materials** → Reinforced throughout the week.  
- 🔍 **Key Reminders & Announcements** → Deadlines, participation expectations, and upcoming discussions.  
- 🔄 **Ongoing Checkpoints Throughout the Week** → Ensuring students stay on track.  

### **🔹 4. Core Literary Study & Thematic Analysis**  
- Introduction to the **literary movement, key figures, and historical context.**  
- **Focused literary concepts and techniques.**  
- **Key Discussion Questions** (Concrete, Abstract, Connecting, Exploratory).  
- **How This Week’s Literature Connects to Previous Weeks.**  

### **🔹 5. Media Exploration & Comparative Analysis**  
- **Examining how the theme manifests in film, music, visual art, and other media.**  
- **Making intertextual connections** between different forms of storytelling.  

### **🔹 6. Practical Application Section**  
- **Bridges theory into real-world application.**  
- **Guided Close Reading Techniques** → Teaching students **how** to annotate and analyze a text effectively.  
- **Step-by-Step Analysis Breakdown** → Ensures students know how to **move from observation to argument.**  
- **Skill-Based Assignments** → Concrete tasks that reinforce **composition, analysis, and interpretation.**  

### **🔹 7. Structured Writing Workshop**  
- **A guided exercise in writing strong literary analysis.**  
- **Applying rhetorical and stylistic techniques from the week’s literature.**  
- **Crafting compelling arguments, structuring evidence, and refining composition.**  

### **🔹 8. Close Reading Assignment Instructions**  
- **A major analytical writing task for the week.**  
- **Detailed breakdown of the assignment & expectations.**  
- **Step-by-step process for constructing a strong close reading.**  

### **🔹 9. Wrap-Up & Reflection**  
- Recap of **major insights from the week’s literature, discussions, and writing.**  
- **Reflection Questions (Concrete, Abstract, Connecting, Exploratory)** that tie into the next week.  
- **Preview of Next Week’s Themes & Readings.**  

---

## **📌 3. TRANSITION FORMATTING & PURPOSE**
- **Each transition must explicitly connect the past, present, and future themes of the class.**  
- Transitions should **remind students what they’ve already learned** and prepare them for **what’s coming next.**  
- **Each transition must reinforce why the section they are about to study matters.**  
- **No section stands alone—each builds into the next, both within the day and across weeks.**  

✅ **Transitions must reference:**  
- **What we just covered.**  
- **Why it matters.**  
- **How it leads into what’s next.**  

🔹 **Example:**  
```markdown
## **Transition: From Theory to Practice – Unmasking the Text**  

We’ve now dissected **the literary devices that make Gothic literature effective**—unreliable narrators, symbolism, irony, eerie settings.  

But how do we apply this knowledge in **real-time analysis**?  

Next, we’ll dive into **practical close reading techniques** that will prepare you for **your own literary dissection of Poe’s work.**  
```

---

## **📌 4. DISCUSSION & QUESTION FORMATTING**
Each discussion section **must contain**:  

### **1️⃣ Concrete Questions (Observation-Based)**
- **Fact-based, directly from the text.**  
- **What do you notice?**  
- **How does a character, setting, or event function in the story?**  

### **2️⃣ Abstract Questions (Concept-Based)**
- **Why does this matter?**  
- **What broader themes or ideas emerge?**  
- **How does this connect to literary theory, philosophy, or culture?**  

### **3️⃣ Connecting Questions (Application-Based)**
- **How does this text relate to other works, real life, or modern media?**  
- **Where do you see similar themes in today’s world?**  

### **4️⃣ Exploratory Questions (Challenging & Speculative)**
- **What if the story had unfolded differently?**  
- **Could the themes of this work still exist in a different setting or era?**  
- **How might another writer approach this same concept?**  

🔹 **Example:**  
```markdown
### **Concrete:**  
- How does the **setting in *The Cask of Amontillado*** enhance the story’s mood?  
- What emotions do **dark, confined spaces** evoke?  

### **Abstract:**  
- How do **Gothic spaces reflect the psychology of characters**?  
- Why do we associate **certain settings with fear or unease**?  

### **Connecting:**  
- Have you ever visited **a place that felt unsettling**? What made it feel that way?  
- Can you think of **a modern book or film** that uses Gothic conventions effectively?  

### **Exploratory:**  
- Could Gothic horror exist **without the supernatural**? Why or why not?  
- How might Gothic literature evolve **in the digital age**?  
```

---

## **📌 5. ERROR CHECKING & FINAL REVIEW PROCESS**
Before finalizing a week’s schedule:  

✅ **Double-check that all sections appear in logical order.**  
✅ **Ensure no content has been omitted from the original compiled schedule.**  
✅ **Confirm transitions effectively connect all elements.**  
✅ **Verify discussion & question formatting is consistent throughout.**  
✅ **Ensure practical applications and skill-based assignments are included.**  

---

### **NEXT STEPS**
Now that we have **our protocols established**, how would you like to proceed?  

1. **Shall I now review the compiled Week 4 schedule for final error-checking?**  
2. **Would you like me to begin refining Week 5 using this framework?**  
3. **Do you want any additional protocols before moving forward?**  

🚀 Let me know how you'd like to proceed!
```

    <link href="https://cdnjs.cloudflare.com/ajax/libs/font-awesome/6.4.0/css/all.min.css" rel="stylesheet">
    <style>
        @import url('https://fonts.googleapis.com/css2?family=Inter:wght@400;500;600;700&display=swap');
        :root {
            --color-primary: #475569; /* Steel Blue/Slate */

            --color-text-light: #6b7280;
        }

            font-family: 'Inter', sans-serif;
            background-color: var(--color-secondary);
            color: var(--color-text);
        }
        .tab-button {
            padding: 1rem 0.25rem;
            border-bottom: 2px solid transparent;
            font-size: 0.875rem;
            font-weight: 500;
            color: var(--color-text-light);
            transition: all 0.2s ease;
            white-space: nowrap;
            cursor: pointer;
        }
        .tab-button:hover {
            color: var(--color-text);
        }
        .tab-button.active {
            border-bottom-color: var(--color-accent);
            color: var(--color-accent);
            font-weight: 600;
        }
        .tab-content { display: none; }
        .tab-content.active { display: block; }
        .table-container { overflow-x: auto; -webkit-overflow-scrolling: touch; }
        table { width: 100%; min-width: 1200px; border-collapse: collapse; }
        th, td { padding: 1rem; text-align: left; vertical-align: top; border-bottom: 1px solid #e5e7eb; }
        th { background-color: #f9fafb; font-weight: 600; color: #374151; cursor: pointer; position: sticky; top: 0; z-index: 10;}
        th .sort-icon { opacity: 0.3; transition: opacity 0.2s; }
        th:hover .sort-icon { opacity: 1; }
        td { background-color: #ffffff; }
        tfoot td { font-weight: 700; background-color: #f9fafb; font-size: 1.125rem; position: sticky; bottom: 0; }
        .phase-header td { background-color: #e0e7ff; color: #3730a3; font-weight: 700; font-size: 1.125rem; }
        .guiding-prompt { color: var(--color-text-light); font-size: 0.875rem; }
        .guiding-prompt p { margin-bottom: 0.5rem; display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center; }
        .guiding-prompt strong { color: var(--color-text); }
        .guiding-prompt select { margin-bottom: 0.75rem; max-width: 150px; }
        .final-estimate { margin-top: 1rem; padding-top: 1rem; border-top: 1px dashed #d1d5db; }
        .calculated-value { font-weight: 600; color: var(--color-accent); font-size: 1.1rem; }
        select, input, button, textarea {
            width: 100%; padding: 0.5rem; border: 1px solid #d1d5db; border-radius: 0.375rem;
            box-shadow: inset 0 1px 2px 0 rgba(0,0,0,0.05); transition: all 0.2s ease;
        }
        button { background-color: var(--color-primary); color: white; font-weight: 600; cursor: pointer; }
        button:hover { background-color: #334155; }
        button.gemini-button {
            background: linear-gradient(135deg, #f97316, #ea580c);
            color: white;
            font-weight: 600;
        }
        button.gemini-button:hover {
            opacity: 0.9;
        }
        #toast {
            position: fixed; bottom: -100px; left: 50%; transform: translateX(-50%);
            background-color: #16a34a; color: white; padding: 1rem 2rem; border-radius: 0.5rem;
            box-shadow: 0 4px 15px rgba(0,0,0,0.2); transition: bottom 0.5s ease-in-out; z-index: 100;
        }
        #toast.show { bottom: 20px; }
        
        /* Modal Styles */
        .modal-overlay {
            position: fixed; top: 0; left: 0; right: 0; bottom: 0;
            background-color: rgba(0, 0, 0, 0.6);
            display: flex; align-items: center; justify-content: center;
            z-index: 1000; opacity: 0; visibility: hidden; transition: all 0.3s ease;
        }
        .modal-overlay.active {
            opacity: 1; visibility: visible;
        }
        .modal-content {
            background-color: white; padding: 2rem; border-radius: 0.75rem;
            box-shadow: 0 10px 25px rgba(0,0,0,0.1);
            width: 90%; max-width: 600px;
            transform: scale(0.95); transition: all 0.3s ease;
        }
        .modal-overlay.active .modal-content {
            transform: scale(1);
        }
    </style>

        <!-- Tabs -->

                <button class="tab-button" data-tab="tab4">Change Log</button>

        <main class="p-6 md:p-8">
            <!-- Tab Contents -->
            <div id="tab0" class="tab-content active">
                 <h2 class="text-xl font-bold text-gray-800 mb-4">Your Progress at a Glance</h2>
                <div class="grid grid-cols-1 md:grid-cols-3 gap-6">
                    <div class="bg-slate-50 p-6 rounded-lg">
                        <h3 class="font-semibold text-gray-500">Total Potential Savings</h3>
                        <p class="text-3xl font-bold text-slate-800 mt-2" id="overview-savings-money">$0 / Yr</p>
                        <p class="text-slate-600" id="overview-savings-hours">~0.0 Hrs / Wk</p>
                    </div>
                    <div class="bg-orange-50 p-6 rounded-lg">
                        <h3 class="font-semibold text-gray-500">High-Priority Tasks</h3>
                        <p class="text-3xl font-bold text-orange-600 mt-2" id="overview-high-priority">0</p>
                        <p class="text-orange-500">Ready to be actioned</p>
                    </div>
                    <div class="bg-indigo-50 p-6 rounded-lg">
                        <h3 class="font-semibold text-gray-500">Approved Changes</h3>
                        <p class="text-3xl font-bold text-indigo-600 mt-2" id="overview-recent-changes">0</p>
                        <p class="text-indigo-500">Logged and ready to implement</p>
                    </div>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div id="tab1" class="tab-content">
                 <div class="bg-blue-50 border-l-4 border-blue-500 text-blue-800 p-4 rounded-r-lg" role="alert">
                    <h3 class="font-bold">Why This Tool?</h3>
                    <p>This tool is designed to cut through the chaos—fewer last-minute runs, fewer missed emails, and more time for what matters. Let’s tackle those small problems that add up and build a smarter, more profitable operation together.</p>
                </div>
                <div class="mt-6">
                    <h3 class="font-semibold text-lg">4 Simple Steps</h3>
                    <ol class="list-decimal list-inside mt-2 space-y-3">
                        <li><strong>Impact Estimators:</strong> Use the dropdowns to answer the guiding questions. The math is done for you automatically.</li>
                        <li><strong>Action Dashboard:</strong> Click <strong>✨ Generate Plan</strong> to get an AI-powered action plan for high-impact issues.</li>
                        <li><strong>Prioritize & Assign:</strong> Use the generated plan to assign owners and set deadlines to turn ideas into action.</li>
                        <li><strong>Change Log:</strong> Propose new ideas. Use <strong>✨ Suggest Reason</strong> to get help from AI.</li>
                    </ol>
                </div>
            </div>
            <div id="tab2" class="tab-content">
                 <div class="table-container">
                     <table>
                         <thead>
                             <tr>
                                 <th class="w-1/5">Proposed Fix & Problem</th>
                                 <th class="w-2/5">Impact Estimator: Wasted Time</th>
                                 <th class="w-2/5">Impact Estimator: Wasted Money</th>
                             </tr>
                         </thead>

            <div id="tab3" class="tab-content">
                 <div class="table-container">
                     <table style="min-width: 1400px;">
                         <thead>
                             <tr>
                                 <th class="w-[5%]">Priority</th>
                                 <th class="w-[15%]">Proposed Fix</th>
                                 <th class="w-[10%]">Est. Savings</th>

                     </table>
                 </div>
            </div>
            <div id="tab4" class="tab-content">
                <div class="grid grid-cols-1 md:grid-cols-2 gap-8">
                    <div>
                        <h2 class="text-xl font-bold text-gray-800 mb-4">Propose a New Change</h2>
                        <form id="change-log-form" class="space-y-4">
                            <div><label for="change-date" class="font-medium">Date</label><input type="date" id="change-date" required></div>
                            <div><label for="change-proposer" class="font-medium">Proposed By</label><input type="text" id="change-proposer" placeholder="Your Name" required></div>
                            <div><label for="change-idea" class="font-medium">Change / New Idea</label><input type="text" id="change-idea" placeholder="e.g., Add 'Weekly Toolbox Talks'" required></div>
                            <div>
                                <label for="change-reason" class="font-medium flex justify-between items-center"><span>Reason</span><button type="button" id="suggest-reason-btn" class="gemini-button text-xs px-2 py-1 w-auto">✨ Suggest Reason</button></label>
                                <textarea id="change-reason" placeholder="e.g., To improve safety" required rows="3"></textarea>
                            </div>
                            <button type="submit">Submit Change for Approval</button>
                        </form>
                    </div>
                    <div>
                        <div class="flex justify-between items-center mb-4">
                             <h2 class="text-xl font-bold text-gray-800">Change History</h2>
                             <div>
                                 <label for="log-filter" class="text-sm font-medium">Filter:</label>
                                 <select id="log-filter" class="text-sm p-1 rounded-md border-gray-300 w-auto">
                                     <option value="all">All</option><option value="pending">Pending</option><option value="approved">Approved</option><option value="rejected">Rejected</option>
                                 </select>
                             </div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="table-container border rounded-lg max-h-96 overflow-y-auto">
                            <table>
                                <thead class="bg-slate-50"><tr><th>Change</th><th>Proposer</th><th>Status</th></tr></thead>
                                <tbody id="change-log-history"></tbody>
                            </table>
                        </div>
                    </div>
                </div>

            The Game Plan | Version 5.0 with Automated Calculations
        </footer>
